//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_771332'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/771332.With_the_Old_Breed?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">With the Old Breed: At Peleliu and Okinawa<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/31280.Eugene_B_Sledge?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Eugene B. Sledge<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.45 avg rating &mdash; 28,966 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1981\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer3423406135611794903\">In his own book, Wartime, Paul Fussell called With the Old Breed \"one of the finest memoirs to emerge from any war.\" John Keegan referred to it in The Second World War as \"one of the most arresting documents in war literature.\" And Studs Terkel was s<\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText3423406135611794903\" style=\"display:none\">In his own book, Wartime, Paul Fussell called With the Old Breed &quot;one of the finest memoirs to emerge from any war.&quot; John Keegan referred to it in The Second World War as &quot;one of the most arresting documents in war literature.&quot; And Studs Terkel was so fascinated with the story he interviewed its author for his book, &quot;The Good War.&quot; What has made E.B. Sledge\'s memoir of his experience fighting in the South Pacific during World War II so devastatingly powerful is its sheer honest simplicity and compassion.\nNow including a new introduction by Paul Fussell, With the Old Breed presents a stirring, personal account of the vitality and bravery of the Marines in the battles at Peleliu and Okinawa. Born in Mobile, Alabama in 1923 and raised on riding, hunting, fishing, and a respect for history and legendary heroes such as George Washington and Daniel Boone, Eugene Bondurant Sledge (later called &quot;Sledgehammer&quot; by his Marine Corps buddies) joined the Marines the year after the bombing of Pearl Harbor and from 1943 to 1946 endured the events recorded in this book. In those years, he passed, often painfully, from innocence to experience.\n\nSledge enlisted out of patriotism, idealism, and youthful courage, but once he landed on the beach at Peleliu, it was purely a struggle for survival. Based on the notes he kept on slips of paper tucked secretly away in his New Testament, he simply and directly recalls those long months, mincing no words and sparing no pain. The reality of battle meant unbearable heat, deafening gunfire, unimaginable brutality and cruelty, the stench of death, and, above all, constant fear. Sledge still has nightmares about &quot;the bloody, muddy month of May on Okinawa.&quot; But, as he also tellingly reveals, the bonds of friendship formed then will never be severed.\n\nSledge\'s honesty and compassion for the other marines, even complete strangers, sets him apart as a memoirist of war. Read as sobering history or as high adventure, With the Old Breed is a moving chronicle of action and courage.<\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"3423406135611794903\" href=\"#\"       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/48305.Cornelius_Ryan">About Cornelius Ryan</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/48305.Cornelius_Ryan"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1332088952p3/48305.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/48305.Cornelius_Ryan">Cornelius Ryan
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
186
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer17317743668446262098">Ryan was born in Dublin. After finishing his education Ryan moved to London in 1940, and became a war correspondent for ''The Daily Telegraph'' in 1941. <br/><br/>He initially covered the air war in Europe during WW II, flew along on fourteen bombing missions with the Eighth Air Force and Ninth Air Force United States Army Air Forces (USAAF), then joined General Patton's Third Third Army and covered its act</span>
  <span id="freeText17317743668446262098" style="display:none">Ryan was born in Dublin. After finishing his education Ryan moved to London in 1940, and became a war correspondent for ''The Daily Telegraph'' in 1941. <br /><br />He initially covered the air war in Europe during WW II, flew along on fourteen bombing missions with the Eighth Air Force and Ninth Air Force United States Army Air Forces (USAAF), then joined General Patton's Third Third Army and covered its actions until the end of the European war. He transferred to the Pacific theater in 1945, and then to Jerusalem in 1946.<br /><br />Ryan emigrated to the United States in 1947 to work for <i>Time</i> magazine, where he reported on the postwar tests of atomic weapons carried out by the United States in the Pacific. This was followed by work for other magazines, including <i>Collier's Weekly</i> and <i>Reader's Digest</i>. <br /><br />He married Kathryn Morgan (1925–1993), a novelist, and became a naturalized citizen of the United States in 1951.<br /> <br />On a trip to Normandy in 1949 Ryan became interested in telling a more complete story of D-Day than had been produced to date. He began compiling information and conducting over 1000 interviews as he gathered stories from both the Allies and the Germans, as well as the French civilians.<br /><br />In 1956 he began to write down his World War II notes for <i>The Longest Day</i>, which tells the story of the invasion of Normandy. Published in 1959 it was an instant success. <br /><br />His next work was <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/84370.The_Last_Battle_The_Classic_History_of_the_Battle_for_Berlin" title="The Last Battle The Classic History of the Battle for Berlin by Cornelius Ryan" rel="nofollow noopener">Last Battle The Classic History of the Battle for Berlin</a> (1966), about the Battle of Berlin. <br /><br />This work was followed by <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/539904.A_Bridge_Too_Far" title="A Bridge Too Far by Cornelius Ryan" rel="nofollow noopener">A Bridge Too Far</a> (1974), which tells the story of Operation Market Garden, the ill-fated assault by allied airborne forces on the Netherlands culminating in the Battle of Arnhem.<br /><br />Ryan was awarded the French Legion of Honor, and an honorary Doctor of Literature degree from Ohio University, where the Cornelius Ryan Collection is housed (Alden Library). He was diagnosed with cancer in 1970, and struggled to finish <i>A Bridge Too Far</i> during his illness. He died in Manhattan, while on tour promoting the book, <i>A Bridge Too Far</i>, only two months after publication.<br /><br />Four years after his death, Ryan's struggle with cancer was detailed in <i>A Private Battle</i> written by his wife, from notes he had secretly left behind for that purpose. He is buried in the Ridgebury Cemetery in northern Ridgefield, Connecticut.<br /><br />Biography info: <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cornelius_Ryan">http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Corneliu...</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="17317743668446262098" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/1413765-the-longest-day">Quotes from The Longest Day</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Wearily, he swung the glasses over to the left again. Slowly, he tracked across the horizon. He reached the dead center of the bay. The glasses stopped moving. Pluskat tensed, stared hard. Through the scattering, thinning mist the horizon was magically filling with ships—ships of every size and description, ships that casually maneuvered back and forth as though they had been there for hours. There appeared to be thousands of them. It was a ghostly armada that somehow had appeared from nowhere. Pluskat stared in frozen disbelief, speechless, moved as he had never been before in his life. At that moment the world of the good soldier Pluskat began falling apart. He says in those first few moments he knew, calmly and surely, that “this was the end for Germany.&rdquo;</span>
